<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome of Solanum pimpinellifolium provides insights into structural variants during tomato breeding"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-11-16"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Solanum pimpinellifolium (SP) is the wild progenitor of cultivated tomato. Because of its remarkable stress tolerance and intense flavor, SP has been used as an important germplasm donor in modern tomato breeding. Here, we present a high-quality chromosome-scale genome sequence of SP LA2093. Genome comparison identifies more than 92,000 structural variants (SVs) between LA2093 and the modern cultivar, Heinz 1706. Genotyping these SVs in ~600 representative tomato accessions identifies alleles under selection during tomato domestication, improvement and modern breeding, and discovers numerous SVs overlapping genes known to regulate important breeding traits such as fruit weight and lycopene content. Expression quantitative trait locus (eQTL) analysis detects hotspots harboring master regulators controlling important fruit quality traits, including cuticular wax accumulation and flavonoid biosynthesis, and SVs contributing to these complex regulatory networks. The LA2093 genome sequence and the identified SVs provide rich resources for future research and biodiversity-based breeding. Solanum pimpinellifolium (SP) is the progenitor of cultivated tomato and an important germplasm. Here, the authors assemble SP genome, identify structural variants (SVs) by comparing with modern cultivar, reveal SVs associated with important breeding traits, and detect SVs harboring master regulators of fruit quality traits."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Solanum pimpinellifolium</i> (SP) is the wild progenitor of cultivated tomato. Because of its remarkable stress tolerance and intense flavor, SP has been used as an important germplasm donor in modern tomato breeding. Here, we present a high-quality chromosome-scale genome sequence of SP LA2093. Genome comparison identifies more than 92,000 structural variants (SVs) between LA2093 and the modern cultivar, Heinz 1706. Genotyping these SVs in ~600 representative tomato accessions identifies alleles under selection during tomato domestication, improvement and modern breeding, and discovers numerous SVs overlapping genes known to regulate important breeding traits such as fruit weight and lycopene content. Expression quantitative trait locus (eQTL) analysis detects hotspots harboring master regulators controlling important fruit quality traits, including cuticular wax accumulation and flavonoid biosynthesis, and SVs contributing to these complex regulatory networks. The LA2093 genome sequence and the identified SVs provide rich resources for future research and biodiversity-based breeding.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>) is the world’s leading vegetable crop with a total production of 182 million tons and a worth over US $60 billion in 2018 (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat">http://www.fao.org/faostat</a>). <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> (SP) carrying red, small, and round fruits is the wild progenitor of the cultivated tomato. It was domesticated in South America to give rise to <i>S. lycopersicum</i> var. <i>cerasiforme</i> (SLC), which was later improved into the big-fruited tomato <i>S. lycopersicum</i> var. <i>lycopersicum</i> (SLL) in Mesoamerica<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Blanca, J. et al. Variation revealed by SNP genotyping and morphology provides insight into the origin of the tomato. PLoS ONE 7, e48198 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d30283e626">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Blanca, J. et al. Genomic variation in tomato, from wild ancestors to contemporary breeding accessions. BMC Genomics 16, 257 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d30283e629">2</a></sup>. The fact that SP can freely hybridize with SLL has enabled the incorporation of SP alleles into modern tomato cultivars to improve disease resistance, abiotic stress tolerance, and other fruit quality traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ebert, A. W. &amp; Schafleitner, R. in Crop Wild Relatives and Climate Change, 141–172 (John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc Hoboken, NJ, USA, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d30283e633">3</a></sup>. Due to the importance of SP, draft genome assemblies of two accessions, LA1589 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d30283e637">4</a></sup>) and LA0480 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Razali, R. et al. The genome sequence of the wild tomato Solanum pimpinellifolium provides insights into salinity tolerance. Front Plant Sci. 9, 1402 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d30283e641">5</a></sup>), have been generated using Illumina short-read sequencing technology. Although these assemblies provided some essential genomic information for SP, they are highly fragmented and incomplete, limiting their applications as robust references in tomato breeding and research. Recently, a chromosome-scale assembly of an SP accession, BGV006775, was generated using Nanopore long reads, providing a valuable resource for the Solanaceae community<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Alonge, M. et al. RaGOO: fast and accurate reference-guided scaffolding of draft genomes. Genome Biol. 20, 224 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d30283e646">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Genomic structural variants (SVs), including insertions/deletions (indels), inversions, and duplications, are the causative genetic variants for many domestication traits of crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Gaut, B. S., Seymour, D. K., Liu, Q. &amp; Zhou, Y. Demography and its effects on genomic variation in crop domestication. Nat. Plants 4, 512–520 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d30283e653">7</a></sup>. Empirical cases in tomato include a ~294-kb inversion at the <i>fas</i> locus leading to enlarged fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Xu, C. et al. A cascade of arabinosyltransferases controls shoot meristem size in tomato. Nat. Genet. 47, 784–792 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d30283e660">8</a></sup>, a 1.4-kb deletion in the <i>CSR</i> gene resulting in an increased fruit weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mu, Q. et al. Fruit weight is controlled by Cell Size Regulator encoding a novel protein that is expressed in maturing tomato fruits. PLoS Genet. 13, e1006930 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d30283e667">9</a></sup>, a partial deletion of the <i>LNK2</i> gene leading to the circadian period lengthening in cultivated tomatoes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Muller, N. A., Zhang, L., Koornneef, M. &amp; Jimenez-Gomez, J. M. Mutations in EID1 and LNK2 caused light-conditional clock deceleration during tomato domestication. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, 7135–7140 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d30283e675">10</a></sup>, and tandem duplications at both <i>sb1</i> and <i>sb3</i> that suppress the excessive inflorescence branching<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Soyk, S. et al. Duplication of a domestication locus neutralized a cryptic variant that caused a breeding barrier in tomato. Nat. Plants 5, 471–479 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d30283e685">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Alonge, M. et al. Major impacts of widespread structural variation on gene expression and crop improvement in tomato. Cell 182, 145–161 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d30283e688">12</a></sup>. Whole-genome SNP data have been employed to reveal the impact of human selection on the tomato genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. 46, 1220–1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d30283e692">13</a></sup> and reconstruct tomato domestication history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Razifard, H. et al. Genomic evidence for complex domestication history of the cultivated tomato in Latin America. Mol. Biol. Evol. 37, 1118–1132 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d30283e697">14</a></sup>. Recently, researchers have started to explore the distribution of SVs in tomato accessions and their population dynamics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Alonge, M. et al. Major impacts of widespread structural variation on gene expression and crop improvement in tomato. Cell 182, 145–161 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d30283e701">12</a></sup>. Identification of SVs between SP and SLL, studying their evolutionary dynamics in different tomato populations and investigating their regulatory roles in the context of gene expression can provide critical insights into the contribution of SVs to important agronomic traits in tomato domestication and breeding.</p><p>In this study, we present a high-quality chromosome-scale genome sequence of an SP accession, LA2093, assembled from PacBio long reads combined with Hi-C chromatin interaction maps. LA2093 harbors many desirable traits and has served as the donor parent of a recombinant inbred line (RIL) population that has been widely used for mapping disease resistance and fruit quality traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ashrafi, H., Kinkade, M. &amp; Foolad, M. R. A new genetic linkage map of tomato based on a Solanum lycopersicum x S. pimpinellifolium RIL population displaying locations of candidate pathogen response genes. Genome 52, 935–956 (2009)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d30283e708">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gonda, I. et al. Sequencing-based bin map construction of a tomato mapping population, facilitating high-resolution quantitative trait loci detection. Plant Genome 12, 1–4 (2019)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d30283e708_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d30283e708_2">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ashrafi, H., Kinkade, M. P., Merk, H. L. &amp; Foolad, M. R. Identification of novel quantitative trait loci for increased lycopene content and other fruit quality traits in a tomato recombinant inbred line population. Mol. Breed. 30, 549–567 (2012)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d30283e708_3">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kinkade, M. P. &amp; Foolad, M. R. Validation and fine mapping of lyc12.1, a QTL for increased tomato fruit lycopene content. Theor. Appl Genet. 126, 2163–2175 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d30283e711">19</a></sup>. We further identify SVs between the genomes of LA2093 and cultivar Heinz 1706 through direct genome comparison combined with PacBio long read mapping, and genotype this reference set of SVs in ~600 tomato accessions representing SP, SLC, and heirloom and modern SLL to determine their population dynamics. We also employ expression quantitative trait locus (eQTL) mapping to explore the roles of SVs in regulating gene expression and identify eQTL hotspots and potential master regulators controlling important fruit traits.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly of the <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> genome</h3><p>We assembled the genome of LA2093 using PacBio long reads and Hi-C chromatin contact information. A total of 96 Gb of PacBio sequences with an N50 read length of 22.3 kb was generated, covering approximately 103× of the LA2093 genome with an estimated size of 923 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">1</a>). The PacBio reads were de novo assembled into contigs, followed by polishing with both PacBio and Illumina reads. This resulted in an assembly of 453 contigs with a total length of 807.6 Mb and an N50 length of 10.9 Mb (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM5">1</a>). A total of 166 million Hi-C read pairs were generated for constructing chromatin interaction maps. These Hi-C contact maps, together with the synteny with the Heinz 1706 genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hosmani, P. S. et al. An improved de novo assembly and annotation of the tomato reference genome using single-molecule sequencing, Hi-C proximity ligation and optical maps. bioRxiv. Preprint at &#xA;                  https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/767764v1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d30283e737">20</a></sup> (version 4.0) and the genetic map constructed using the NC EBR-1 × LA2093 RIL population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Gonda, I. et al. Sequencing-based bin map construction of a tomato mapping population, facilitating high-resolution quantitative trait loci detection. Plant Genome 12, 1–4 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d30283e741">16</a></sup>, were used to scaffold the assembled contigs. Finally, 385 contigs with a total length of ~800 Mb, accounting for 99.0% of the assembly, were clustered into 12 pseudomolecules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig1">1a</a>). The Hi-C heatmap (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">2</a>) and the good collinearity between the pseudomolecules and the genetic map (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">3</a>) supported the chromosome-scale structure of the assembly. BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d30283e755">21</a></sup> assessment indicated that about 97.8% of the core conserved plant genes were found complete in the LA2093 assembly (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM5">1</a>). Further evaluation using Merqury<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Rhie, A., Walenz, B. P., Koren, S. &amp; Phillippy, A. M. Merqury: reference-free quality, completeness, and phasing assessment for genome assemblies. Genome Biol. 21, 245 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d30283e762">22</a></sup> revealed a consensus quality score (QV) of 46 for the LA2093 assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">1</a>). Collectively, these results indicated that the LA2093 genome assembly is of high quality, comparable to or better than the BGV006775 assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Alonge, M. et al. RaGOO: fast and accurate reference-guided scaffolding of draft genomes. Genome Biol. 20, 224 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d30283e770">6</a></sup> but with substantially improved contiguity and completeness compared to the two previously reported <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d30283e777">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Razali, R. et al. The genome sequence of the wild tomato Solanum pimpinellifolium provides insights into salinity tolerance. Front Plant Sci. 9, 1402 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d30283e780">5</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM5">1</a>). A total of 544.3 Mb repetitive sequences (67.3%) were identified in the LA2093 assembly with the <i>gypsy</i> retrotransposon being the most abundant repeat family (37.8%; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">2</a>). The genome was predicted to harbor 35,761 protein-coding genes, of which 35,535 (99.4%) were supported by RNA-Seq data, and/or homologs in the NCBI non-redundant protein database.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genomic landscape of S. pimpinellifolium LA2093 and structural variants identified between LA2093 and Heinz 1706."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Genomic landscape of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> LA2093 and structural variants identified between LA2093 and Heinz 1706.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="359" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Features of the LA2093 genome. (i) Ideogram of the 12 chromosomes in Mb scale. (ii) Repeat content (% nucleotides per Mb); (iii) gene density (number of genes per Mb). (iv) Gene expression (FPKM). (v) Densities of SVs (structural variants) (outer) and SNPs (inner) in comparison to Heinz 1706 (number of SVs and SNPs per Mb). <b>b</b> Alignment of chromosome 1 between LA2093 and Heinz 1706. The color intensity in Hi-C heatmaps represents the number of links between two 100-kb windows. The inversion shown in blue (left) is supported by high-density contacts pointed by the two blue arrows in Hi-C heatmaps generated from Heinz 1706 Hi-C reads aligned to the LA2093 genome (middle), while no corresponding contract is found in the LA3093 Hi-C heatmap (right). <b>c</b> Distribution of SV sizes. <b>d</b> Contents of different categories of transposable elements in SV regions and the whole-genome of LA2093.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genomic SVs between LA2093 and Heinz 1706</h3><p>Alignment of the genome sequences of LA2093 and Heinz 1706 (version 4.0) showed good collinearity between the two reference genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">4</a>). Despite the high collinearity, 28 inversions ranging from 483 bp to 13.9 Mb were identified distributing across all 12 chromosomes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM6">2</a>), which were further supported by PacBio reads and/or Hi-C maps (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">5</a>). Together these inversions harbored approximately 800 genes (861 in LA2093 and 733 in Heinz 1706), of which a large portion (457) were annotated with functions related to disease resistance and response to abiotic stress (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM7">3</a>).</p><p>In addition to inversions, indels between LA2093 and Heinz 1706 genomes were detected by direct comparison of the two high-quality assemblies combined with mapping of PacBio long reads to the genomes. A total of 92,523 indels, ranging from 10 bp to 2.4 Mb, were identified (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig1">1a, c</a> and Data 4). As expected, the majority of the indels were relatively short with 82.8% &lt;100 bp and only 0.1% &gt;100 kb (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig1">1c</a>). Approximately 52.9% of these indel sequences were <i>gypsy</i>-like retrotransposons, compared to 37.8% of the entire genome, while the contents of other types of transposable elements were similar between the indel regions and the whole-genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig1">1d</a>), suggesting that indels occurred more frequently in genome regions occupied by <i>gypsy</i>-like retrotransposons.</p><p>SVs in gene body and promoter regions can impact gene functions and expression. Only 14.8% of identified indels overlapped with gene body or promoter (defined here as 3-kb upstream of gene body) regions, notably lower than the proportion in the whole-genome (27.7%), implying a functional constraint against these indels on coding or regulatory regions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">3</a>). More than half of the predicted genes in LA2093 (21,875 out of 35,761) and Heinz 1706 (19,590 out of 34,689) had at least one indel in their gene body or promoter regions, with 2,419 in LA2093 and 1,710 in Heinz 1706 having indels in their coding sequences (CDS) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">6</a>). Genes affected by the identified indels were enriched with those involved in response to stimulus, reproduction, signal transduction, and primary and secondary metabolic processes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">7</a>), suggesting that these indels may contribute to the differences in disease resistance and fruit quality traits between the wild and cultivated tomatoes. We detected several SVs known to underlie tomato domestication traits, such as the 1.4-kb deletion and 22-bp insertion in the <i>CSR</i> gene leading to increased fruit weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mu, Q. et al. Fruit weight is controlled by Cell Size Regulator encoding a novel protein that is expressed in maturing tomato fruits. PLoS Genet. 13, e1006930 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d30283e888">9</a></sup>, the 7-kb deletion in the <i>LNK2</i> gene responsible for the circadian period lengthening in cultivated tomatoes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Muller, N. A., Zhang, L., Koornneef, M. &amp; Jimenez-Gomez, J. M. Mutations in EID1 and LNK2 caused light-conditional clock deceleration during tomato domestication. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, 7135–7140 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d30283e896">10</a></sup>, the 4-kb substitution in the promoter of the <i>TomLoxC</i> gene, which contributes to fruit flavor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d30283e903">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Shen, J. et al. A 13-lipoxygenase, TomloxC, is essential for synthesis of C5 flavour volatiles in tomato. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 419–428 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d30283e906">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chen, G. et al. Identification of a specific isoform of tomato lipoxygenase (TomloxC) involved in the generation of fatty acid-derived flavor compounds. Plant Physiol. 136, 2641–2651 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d30283e909">24</a></sup>, and the 85-bp deletion in the promoter of the <i>ENO</i> gene, which regulates floral meristem activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Yuste-Lisbona, F. J. et al. ENO regulates tomato fruit size through the floral meristem development network. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 117, 8187–8195 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d30283e916">25</a></sup> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Selection of SVs in tomato domestication and breeding</h3><p>De novo detection of SVs based on short read alignments to a reference genome is subject to a relatively high rate of both false negatives and false positives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Handsaker, R. E., Korn, J. M., Nemesh, J. &amp; McCarroll, S. A. Discovery and genotyping of genome structural polymorphism by sequencing on a population scale. Nat. Genet 43, 269–276 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d30283e932">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mills, R. E. et al. Mapping copy number variation by population-scale genome sequencing. Nature 470, 59–65 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d30283e935">27</a></sup>. Therefore, the SVs identified between LA2093 and Heinz 1706 through direct genome comparison and PacBio long read mapping provided a valuable set of reference SVs that could be used to investigate the roles of SVs in tomato domestication and breeding. The 92,523 indels and 28 inversions were genotyped in 597 tomato accessions, including 51 SP, 6 <i>S. cheesmaniae</i> and <i>S. galapagense</i> (SCG), 228 SLC, 226 heirloom, 52 modern and 34 other cultivars (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM10">6</a>). To estimate the accuracy of SV genotyping using short read mapping to the reference SVs, we generated 31.1 Gb of Nanopore long read sequences for an SP accession, LA1589, and aligned the long reads to the LA2093 and Heinz 1706 genomes for SV calling. About 96.2% of the genotypes determined using the LA1589 short reads were confirmed by the Nanopore long read mapping method. Genotyping the SVs in the two reference accessions using Illumina short reads further supported a high specificity of our SV genotyping (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">4</a>). Phylogenetic and population structure analyses using the SVs clearly separated the SP and SCG groups from the heirloom and modern groups with SLC being the intermediate group between the wild and cultivated accessions. A similar pattern was observed using whole-genome SNPs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">8</a>), further assuring the high specificity of our identified SVs. Seven accessions positioned into unexpected species groups were excluded from downstream analyses (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM10">6</a>).</p><p>SV allele frequency changes among different tomato populations are a result of evolutionary events, such as selection of desirable traits, reduced population size, and introgression from ancestral groups. To identify SVs under selection during tomato domestication and breeding, we investigated SV allele frequency changes from SP to SLC for domestication, from SLC to SLL heirlooms for improvement, and from heirlooms to modern elite lines for modern breeding. In the SP population, SV loci with the homozygous LA2093 alleles were prevalent, making up an average of 58.4% of the SVs in each accession, while only 39.4% SV loci had the homozygous Heinz 1706 genotypes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig2">2a</a>). After domestication and improvement, the frequencies of Heinz 1706 alleles increased to 84.3% and 95.3% in SLC and heirloom tomatoes, respectively, and then slightly decreased to 93.6% in modern lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig2">2a</a>). These findings implied substantial genetic diversity loss imposed by domestication, especially the loss of the SP-specific alleles. The allele frequencies of 38,367 SVs were significantly changed between different tomato populations (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM8">4</a>). During domestication, the LA2093 allele frequencies of 17 inversions and 37,632 indels were significantly lower in SLC than in SP, while only 217 indels had the LA2093 allele frequencies significantly higher in SLC (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig2">2b</a>). These selected indels could affect 14,189 genes in LA2093 and 12,264 in Heinz 1706. In the improvement process, only 103 indels had higher LA2093 allele frequencies in heirlooms than in SLC, and 25,579 SVs (13 inversions and 25,566 indels) displayed significantly lower LA2093 allele frequencies in heirlooms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig2">2b</a>), which collectively could affect 9530 and 7870 genes in LA2093 and Heinz 1706, respectively. The enriched functions were shared between genes affected by the SVs selected during domestication and improvement, including stress and stimulus response, biosynthesis, cell differentiation, embryo development, pollination, and reproduction processes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig2">2c, d</a>). These results demonstrated a common selection preference for the Heinz 1706 alleles in tomato domestication and improvement. It is worth noting that, despite the continuous loss of wild species alleles from SP to SLC and SLL, 1397 SVs exhibited significantly higher LA2093 allele frequencies in modern lines than in heirlooms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig2">2b</a>). This could be related to the re-introduction of agriculturally favorable alleles from wild accessions into modern SLL lines.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="SVs under selection during tomato domestication and breeding."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: SVs under selection during tomato domestication and breeding.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="489" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Percentages of SVs with different genotypes in each accession of different groups. The numbers of accessions are 50, 6, 224, 225, and 51 in SP, SCG, SLC, Heirloom, and Modern groups, respectively. For each box plot, the lower and upper bounds of the box indicate the first and third quartiles, respectively, and the center line indicates the median. The whisker represents 1.5× interquartile range of the lower or upper quartile. <b>b</b> Venn diagrams of selected SVs during domestication (SP to SLC), improvement (SLC to heirloom) and modern breeding (heirloom to modern). <b>c</b>, <b>d</b> GO terms enriched in genes affected by SVs selected during domestication (<b>c</b>) and improvement (<b>d</b>). Enriched GO terms were identified using two-tailed Fisher’s exact test, adjusted for multiple comparisons. Source data underlying Fig. 2a are provided as a Source data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The nucleotide diversity in the modern group was similar to that of the heirlooms but lower than that in SLC and SP for most genomic regions. However, a substantially higher nucleotide diversity level was observed in the modern group compared to the heirlooms on chromosomes 4, 5, and 11, consistent with the higher <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values between the two populations on these chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">9a, b</a>). Concordantly, introgressions of genomic regions from SP to modern cultivars were identified in all these three chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">9c</a>), which are largely consistent with those identified in a recent study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Alonge, M. et al. Major impacts of widespread structural variation on gene expression and crop improvement in tomato. Cell 182, 145–161 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1033">12</a></sup>. The introgressions on chromosome 4 contained a cold tolerance QTL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Foolad, M. R., Chen, F. Q. &amp; Lin, G. Y. RFLP mapping of QTLs conferring cold tolerance during seed germination in an interspecific cross of tomato. Mol. Breed. 4, 519–529 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1037">28</a></sup>, on chromosome 5 carried several known QTLs controlling soluble solid content (SSC) in fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. 46, 1220–1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1042">13</a></sup>, and on chromosome 11 included an important disease resistant genes, <i>Sm</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Su, X. et al. Fine mapping and molecular marker development of the Sm gene conferring resistance to gray leaf spot (Stemphylium spp.) in tomato. Theor. Appl Genet 132, 871–882 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1048">29</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">9d</a>). These results imply that SP introgressions in the modern cultivars might be acquired through contemporary breeding to introduce abiotic and biotic stress tolerance and favorable flavor traits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">SV selection associated with breeding traits</h3><p>Tomato fruit phenotypes have changed dramatically during domestication and improvement. Investigating population dynamics of SVs potentially affecting the expression or functions of genes controlling important horticultural traits can improve our understanding of the impacts of human selection on these genes and provide potential targets for breeding. Fruit size enlargement is a major domestication syndrome in tomato. Several SVs have been identified to be associated with fruit weight, including the 1.4-kb deletion in <i>CSR</i> and the 85-bp deletion in the promoter of <i>ENO</i>, both of which underlie larger fruit size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mu, Q. et al. Fruit weight is controlled by Cell Size Regulator encoding a novel protein that is expressed in maturing tomato fruits. PLoS Genet. 13, e1006930 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1069">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Yuste-Lisbona, F. J. et al. ENO regulates tomato fruit size through the floral meristem development network. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 117, 8187–8195 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1072">25</a></sup>. Consistent with that reported in Mu et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mu, Q. et al. Fruit weight is controlled by Cell Size Regulator encoding a novel protein that is expressed in maturing tomato fruits. PLoS Genet. 13, e1006930 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1076">9</a></sup>, we found that the allele frequency of the 1.4-kb deletion in <i>CSR</i> was 1.0% and 11.5% in SP and SLC, respectively, and became largely fixed in SLL with 90.2% in heirlooms and 94.1% in modern lines (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>). The 85-bp deletion in the <i>ENO</i> promoter had a frequency of 54.7% in SP, 90.8% in SLC, 94.0% in heirloom and 96.8% in modern accessions. Interestingly, we identified an additional 257-bp insertion in <i>CSR</i> spanning the 5′ UTR and the CDS, which had a frequency of 38.6% in SP, 77.9% in SLC, 98.3% in heirloom and 97.7% in modern accessions, and another 57-bp deletion in the <i>ENO</i> promoter, whose allele frequencies in SP, SLC, SLL heirloom and modern were 22.0%, 83.5%, 92.3%, and 94.6%, respectively. Furthermore, indels were detected for additional fruit weight genes, including a 23-bp insertion in the promoter of <i>RRA3a</i>, which had an allele frequency pattern that was suggestive of selection during domestication (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>).</p><p>Fruit lycopene levels seem to have decreased upon the origin of SLC and then have largely remained in SLL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Razifard, H. et al. Genomic evidence for complex domestication history of the cultivated tomato in Latin America. Mol. Biol. Evol. 37, 1118–1132 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1105">14</a></sup>. We identified indels in genes that control lycopene biosynthesis and cyclization, of which the Heinz 1706 allele frequencies were significantly increased from SP to SLC and reached near fixation in heirlooms (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>). These Heinz 1706 alleles, mostly deletions in promoters, were associated with decreased expression of <i>1‐deoxy‐D‐xylulose 5‐phosphate synthase</i> (<i>DXS</i>), <i>1-deoxy-D-xylulose-5-phosphate reductoisomerase</i> (<i>DXR</i>), <i>geranylgeranyl pyrophosphate synthase 2</i> (<i>GGPPS2</i>) and <i>ζ-carotene desaturase</i> (<i>ZDS</i>), and higher expression of <i>LCY-B</i> in the ripening fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig3">3</a>), suggesting that these mutations might cause reduced lycopene levels in SLC and SLL by downregulating lycopene biosynthetic genes, and promoting lycopene degradation through upregulation of <i>LCY-B</i>. Interestingly, we found a recovery of certain LA2093 alleles in the elite tomato lines, including the ones potentially associated with higher expression of <i>DXR</i> and <i>GGPPS2</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Selected SVs affecting the expression of lycopene metabolism genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Selected SVs affecting the expression of lycopene metabolism genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="297" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Lycopene biosynthesis and degradation pathway in tomato. Stars indicate enzyme-coding genes having selected SVs associated with significantly different gene expression levels between the two alleles (two-tailed Student’s <i>t</i>-test <i>p</i> value &lt;0.01). <b>b</b> Allele frequencies of selected SVs in SP (P), SLC (C), heirloom (H) and modern (M) populations. <b>c</b> Gene expression levels in tomato accessions carrying the homozygous LA2093 and Heinz 1706 alleles, respectively, of the selected SVs. For each SV from left to right, the numbers of accessions with homozygous LA2093 alleles are 11, 20, 4, 10, 9, 9, and 18, and those with homozygous Heinz alleles 162, 210, 131, 129, 164, 155, and 244, respectively. Two-tailed Student’s <i>t</i>-test was performed to compare expression levels of each gene between the accessions with homozygous LA2093 and with Heinz alleles for each SV. For each box plot, the lower and upper bounds of the box indicate the first and third quartiles, respectively, and the center line indicates the median. The whisker represents 1.5× interquartile range of the lower or upper quartile. G3P, glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate; IPP, isopentenyl diphosphate; DMAPP, dimethylallyl diphosphate; GGPP, geranylgeranyl diphosphate; DXS, 1-deoxy-d-xylulose 5-phosphate synthase; DXR, 1-deoxy-D-xylulose-5-phosphate reductoisomerase; GGPPS, geranylgeranyl pyrophosphate synthase; PSY, phytoene synthase; ZISO, ζ-carotene isomerase; ZDS, ζ-carotene desaturase; CrtISO, carotene isomerase; LCY-B, lycopene β-cyclase; LCY-E, lycopene ε-cyclase. Source data underlying Fig. 3c are provided as a Source data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Fruit ripening regulation is of great interest to tomato researchers and breeders. Tomato FUL1, FUL2, and TAGL1 play key roles in controlling fruit ripening by interacting with RIN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. A MADS-box gene necessary for fruit ripening at the tomato ripening-inhibitor (rin) locus. Science 296, 343–346 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1196">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bemer, M. et al. The tomato FRUITFULL homologs TDR4/FUL1 and MBP7/FUL2 regulate ethylene-independent aspects of fruit ripening. Plant Cell 24, 4437–4451 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1199">31</a></sup>. Here, we identified a 15-bp in-frame deletion (SV28464) in the CDS of <i>FUL1</i> of Heinz 1706, which had frequencies of 1.0%, 75.5%, and 99.0% in SP, SLC, and heirlooms, respectively. Intriguingly, <i>FUL1</i> was expressed at a considerably higher level in ripe fruits of the elite tomato line, NC EBR-1, carrying the derived deletion allele, than in LA2093 that carries the non-deletion allele (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">10a</a>). Consistently, accessions with homozygous <i>FUL1</i> deletion allele expressed the gene at a higher level in the orange-stage fruit than those with the non-deletion allele (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">10b</a>). Together, these results suggested that the <i>FUL1</i> deletion allele was associated with the higher <i>FUL1</i> expression and positively selected during domestication. <i>FUL2</i> of Heinz 1706 carried three SVs [an 87-bp deletion (SV28464), a 22-bp deletion (SV28465) and a 15-bp insertion (SV28466)] in its promoter. SV28464 had frequencies of 46.3%, 86.7% and 98.4%, SV28465 24.3%, 88.9% and 99.4%, and SV28466 14.3%, 85.1% and 96.2% in SP, SLC and heirlooms, respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>), suggesting their positive selection during tomato domestication and/or improvement. All these three SVs were associated with the reduced expression of <i>FUL2</i> in the orange-stage fruit (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">10b</a>). The seemingly opposite effects of the selected alleles on the expression of <i>FUL1</i> and <i>FUL2</i> in orange-stage fruit could be related to the different spatial and temporal expression patterns of <i>FUL1</i> and <i>FUL2</i> in ripening fruit and their specific developmental functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bemer, M. et al. The tomato FRUITFULL homologs TDR4/FUL1 and MBP7/FUL2 regulate ethylene-independent aspects of fruit ripening. Plant Cell 24, 4437–4451 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1250">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang, S. et al. Members of the tomato FRUITFULL MADS-box family regulate style abscission and fruit ripening. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 3005–3014 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1253">32</a></sup>. In addition, one 38-bp deletion (SV58448) and one 28-bp insertion (SV58449) were found in the last intron of <i>TAGL1</i> of Heinz 1706, of which SV58448 had frequencies of 1.1%, 55.4% and 85.2% and SV58449 32.3%, 63.0% and 84.1% in SP, SLC and heirlooms, respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>). In addition, the 28-bp deletion was found to be associated with higher expression of <i>TAGL1</i> in cultivated tomatoes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">10b</a>).</p><p>Wild species have been used as a source for improving SSC in cultivated tomatoes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Petreikov, M. et al. Temporally extended gene expression of the ADP-Glc pyrophosphorylase large subunit (AgpL1) leads to increased enzyme activity in developing tomato fruit. Planta 224, 1465–1479 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1273">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Fridman, E., Carrari, F., Liu, Y. S., Fernie, A. R. &amp; Zamir, D. Zooming in on a quantitative trait for tomato yield using interspecific introgressions. Science 305, 1786–1789 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1276">34</a></sup>. Three well-characterized genes that contribute to fruit SSC, include <i>SUCR</i> controlling sucrose accumulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Chetelat, R. T., Deverna, J. W. &amp; Bennett, A. B. Effects of the Lycopersicon chmielewskii sucrose accumulator gene (sucr) on fruit yield and quality parameters following introgression into tomato. Theor. Appl Genet 91, 334–339 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1283">35</a></sup>, and <i>Lin5</i> and <i>Agp-L1</i> regulating hexose content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Fridman, E., Carrari, F., Liu, Y. S., Fernie, A. R. &amp; Zamir, D. Zooming in on a quantitative trait for tomato yield using interspecific introgressions. Science 305, 1786–1789 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1294">34</a></sup>. We found a 13-bp insertion (SV26359) in the first intron of <i>SUCR</i> associated with lower expression of the gene (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">11</a>), whose frequencies were 13.3% in SP, 86.5% in SLC, 99.4% in heirloom and fixed in modern accessions. LA2093 carried a 15-bp in-frame deletion in <i>Lin5</i> (SV67476), resulting in a 5-aa deletion at one amino acid upstream of the critical amino acid for sucrose binding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Fridman, E., Carrari, F., Liu, Y. S., Fernie, A. R. &amp; Zamir, D. Zooming in on a quantitative trait for tomato yield using interspecific introgressions. Science 305, 1786–1789 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1307">34</a></sup>. The frequency of the non-deletion allele was substantially increased during domestication (33.7% in SP, 90.9% in SLC, 98.9% in heirloom and fixed in modern accessions). For <i>AgpL1</i>, we identified an 18-bp deletion in the second intron (SV12806) and a 33-bp deletion in the promoter (SV12807), both having significantly increased allele frequencies in SLC and SLL compared to SP (37.5% in SP, 85.2% in SLC and 96.2% in heirloom for the 18-bp deletion, and 32.7% in SP, 85.2% in SLC and 96.3% in heirloom for the 33-bp deletion; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>). Domestication traits, including high fruit yield, increased fruit:leaf ratio and determinant habit, are found to be negatively correlated with high SSC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Chetelat, R. T., Deverna, J. W. &amp; Bennett, A. B. Effects of the Lycopersicon chmielewskii sucrose accumulator gene (sucr) on fruit yield and quality parameters following introgression into tomato. Theor. Appl Genet 91, 334–339 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1318">35</a></sup>. Although the Heinz 1706 alleles of these SSC genes were nearly fixed in the modern tomatoes, possibly a result of hitchhiking with domestication and improvement traits, the LA2093 alleles identified here offer an opportunity to improve SSC in the elite lines.</p><p>Incorporating alleles from wild species into SLL has been a strategy in tomato breeding to improve disease resistance. We identified selected SVs in a number of well-studied disease resistance genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>). Further functional characterization of these SVs may open a door to recover the disease-resistance traits in cultivated tomatoes. We also identified selected SVs for genes involving in hormonal regulation, flower, inflorescence, seed and leaf development, as well metabolite biosynthesis (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>), which would be associated with the dramatic changes of morphotype and metabolite diversity during the long history of tomato breeding.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome-wide mapping of eQTLs</h3><p>To explore the roles of SVs in gene expression regulation, we performed eQTL analysis using the published orange-stage fruit transcriptome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhu, G. et al. Rewiring of the fruit metabolome in tomato breeding. Cell 172, 249–261.e12. (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1341">36</a></sup>. A total of 46,848 SVs in 10,789 eQTL regions were identified to be significantly associated with the expression of 5595 genes, including 2708 (25.1%) cis- and 8081 (74.9%) trans-eQTLs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig4">4a</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM11">7</a><b>)</b>. The cis-eQTLs were more significantly associated with the gene expression and thus explained more expression variation than the trans-eQTLs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">12a, b</a>). The lead SVs of most cis-eQTLs were located near the start or end of coding regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">12c</a>), indicating the important regulatory roles of sequences in these regions on gene expression.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genome-wide mapping of eQTLs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Genome-wide mapping of eQTLs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19682-0/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19682_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="774" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Positions of eQTLs identified in the genome. <b>b</b> Trans-eQTL hotspots. The outermost circle displays ideograms of the 12 tomato chromosomes. The second circle shows the number of target genes for all eQTLs in each 2-Mb window. The third circle shows the number of target genes of each trans-eQTL hotspot. The innermost circle shows the links between three interesting eQTL hotspots and their target genes. Links between the three hotspots harboring the master regulators, A20/AN1 zinc finger protein, MYB12, and WRI3, respectively, and their target genes are depicted in purple, blue and green, respectively. <b>c</b> Expression profiles of WRI3-targeted lipid biosynthetic genes in different tomato tissues. The <i>WRI3</i> gene (<i>SPIMP03g0114120</i>) is highlighted in red. <b>d</b> Correlation coefficients between the expression levels of genes in the <i>WRI3</i>-hotspot and target genes. <i>WRI3</i> and the target lipid biosynthetic genes are highlighted in red. <b>e</b> Manhattan plot of eQTLs associated with the <i>WRI3</i> expression. The horizontal dashed lines correspond to the Bonferroni-corrected significance thresholds at α = 0.05 and α = 1.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The genomic distribution of trans-eQTLs was assessed to identify eQTL hotspots underlying the major regulatory variations across the tomato accessions. A total of 48 hotspots regulating the expression of 554 genes, as well as the potential master regulator within each hotspot, were identified (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM12">8</a>). One hotspot located on chromosome 1 around 76.5 Mb (<i>P</i> = 1.68 × 10<sup>−17</sup>) contained the <i>MYB12</i> gene, which encodes a key regulator of flavonoid biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhu, G. et al. Rewiring of the fruit metabolome in tomato breeding. Cell 172, 249–261.e12. (2018)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1428">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Adato, A. et al. Fruit-surface flavonoid accumulation in tomato is controlled by a SlMYB12-regulated transcriptional network. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000777 (2009)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1428_1">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ballester, A. R. et al. Biochemical and molecular analysis of pink tomatoes: deregulated expression of the gene encoding transcription factor SlMYB12 leads to pink tomato fruit color. Plant Physiol. 152, 71–84 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1431">38</a></sup>. Among the 18 genes whose expression was regulated by this hotspot, seven encoded flavonoid biosynthetic enzymes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM13">9</a>), including chalcone synthase and flavonoid 3′-hydroxylase, which have been proved to be the direct targets of MYB12 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Multi-level engineering facilitates the production of phenylpropanoid compounds in tomato. Nat. Commun. 6, 8635 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1438">39</a></sup>). Interestingly, another hotspot (Chr1:18800715-19894697; <i>P</i> = 2 × 10<sup>−11</sup>) was found to affect the expression of the flavonoid biosynthetic genes regulated by the MYB12 hotspot. This hotspot contained an A20/AN1 zinc finger gene, whose homolog in poplar mediates flavonoid biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, J. et al. A stress-associated protein, PtSAP13, from Populus trichocarpa provides tolerance to salt stress. Int J. Mol. Sci. 20, 5782 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1447">40</a></sup>. Furthermore, a total of 17 additional eQTLs were detected for 17 flavonoid biosynthetic genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">13</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM14">10</a>), among which 14 genes had trans-eQTLs and three had cis-eQTLs. Further study of these eQTLs will likely advance our knowledge on the gene regulatory network of flavonoid biosynthesis.</p><p>A major hotspot was found on chromosome 3 with 26 target genes, 14 of which were associated with lipid metabolism (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM15">11</a>). An AP2/ERF transcription factor orthologous to Arabidopsis <i>WRI3</i> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="To, A. et al. WRINKLED transcription factors orchestrate tissue-specific regulation of fatty acid biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 24, 5007–5023 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1467">41</a></sup>), was identified as the master regulator targeting 17 genes, including those encoding pyruvate kinase, pyruvate dehydrogenase, acetyl-coenzyme A carboxylase, carboxyl transferase and acyl carrier proteins (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM15">11</a>). Co-expression of the tomato <i>WRI3</i> with lipid metabolism genes in the epidermis of developing fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Shinozaki, Y. et al. High-resolution spatiotemporal transcriptome mapping of tomato fruit development and ripening. Nat. Commun. 9, 364 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1478">42</a></sup> suggested its potential function in tissue-specific regulation of lipid biosynthesis, thus contributing to fruit cuticular wax accumulation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig4">4c</a>). The expression of <i>WRI3</i> was positively correlated with that of all its target genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig4">4d</a>), suggesting its positive regulatory role. Furthermore, eQTL analysis identified two SVs, a 11-bp insertion in the sixth intron and a 77-bp deletion located in the promoter of <i>WRI3</i>, that were significantly associated (<i>P</i> value = 1.93e−12) with its expression (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Fig4">4e</a><b>;</b> Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">14</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM9">5</a>). Altogether, these results suggested that <i>WRI3</i> could be a key regulator of fruit outer epidermis lipid deposition.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p><i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> is thought to be the wild progenitor of cultivated tomatoes and offers desirable agronomic traits for breeding. The currently available chromosomal-scale SP assemblies were built based on the Heinz 1706 reference<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Alonge, M. et al. RaGOO: fast and accurate reference-guided scaffolding of draft genomes. Genome Biol. 20, 224 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1523">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Alonge, M. et al. Major impacts of widespread structural variation on gene expression and crop improvement in tomato. Cell 182, 145–161 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1526">12</a></sup>. In this study, by utilizing PacBio long read sequencing, Hi-C chromatin contact maps and genetic maps, we provide the high-quality pseudomolecules of the SP genome that complements the existing tomato Heinz 1706 reference and serves as the foundation for exploring the genetic potential of SP and studying the genome evolution from wild to cultivated tomato under human selection. Explicit comparison between the genomes of LA2093 and Heinz 1706 enabled us to discover megabase-scale large SVs that could not be detected previously due to limitation of sequencing read length<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Alonge, M. et al. Major impacts of widespread structural variation on gene expression and crop improvement in tomato. Cell 182, 145–161 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1530">12</a></sup>. By investigating the frequency changes of wild alleles at the identified SV loci between LA2093 and Heinz 1706 in a large collection of ~600 tomato accessions representing SP, SLC, heirloom, and modern SLL, we not only revealed the population dynamics of known causal SVs underlying important traits during tomato domestication, improvement, and modern breeding, but also identified numerous candidate SVs in or near well-characterized genes controlling horticultural traits. The population dynamics of these SVs provide useful information for future determination of their likelihood of being the causal genetic variants. Furthermore, these SVs may be used as potential targets for future breeding programs to improve fruit quality and stress tolerance.</p><p>The LA2093 alleles of most selected SVs had significantly reduced frequencies after domestication and improvement. The massive loss of these wild alleles in cultivated tomatoes could be related to the large selective sweeps associated with domestication and improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. 46, 1220–1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1537">13</a></sup>. Similar to the finding of metabolic genes hitchhiked with <i>fw11.3</i> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhu, G. et al. Rewiring of the fruit metabolome in tomato breeding. Cell 172, 249–261.e12. (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1544">36</a></sup>), linkage drag also contributed to the recovery of many LA2093 alleles in modern cultivars on chromosomes 4, 5, and 11, likely associated with recent intentional introduction of disease resistance, stress tolerance, and favorable fruit quality traits into cultivars from wild accessions. Large structural variations complicate the cloning of genes underlying target traits and introgression of wild alleles in breeding without bringing along unwanted hitchhikers. Our study provides ample information that can be used to facilitate the recovery of wild beneficial alleles into modern elite cultivars.</p><p>eQTL analysis helped to unravel the hierarchical regulatory relationships among genes and provided insights into the effects of SVs on gene expression.
