In addition to confirming the central role of MYB12 in regulating flavonoid biosynthesis in tomato fruit, we identified additional eQTLs containing potential regulatory genes and SVs contributing to the complex regulatory network of flavonoid biosynthesis. Tomato fruit cuticular lipids provide a barrier against water loss and microbial infection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Isaacson, T. et al. Cutin deficiency in the tomato fruit cuticle consistently affects resistance to microbial infection and biomechanical properties, but not transpirational water loss. Plant J. 60, 363–377 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1551">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Martin, L. B. &amp; Rose, J. K. There’s more than one way to skin a fruit: formation and functions of fruit cuticles. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 4639–4651 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1554">44</a></sup>. An AP2/ERF transcription factor gene, <i>WRI3</i>, may serve as a master regulator that controls the tissue-specific expression of key lipid biosynthetic enzyme genes in tomato fruit epidermis. These results increased our knowledge of the regulatory mechanism involved in fruit cuticular lipid accumulation, which could be applied to the development of crops with improved post-harvest performance.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Plants of the <i>Solanum pimpinellifolium</i> accession LA2093 were grown in the greenhouse at Boyce Thompson Institute in Ithaca, New York, with a 16-h light period at 20 °C (night) to 25 °C (day).</p><p>A PacBio SMRT library was constructed from the high molecular weight DNA following the standard SMRTbell library preparation protocol and sequenced on a PacBio Sequel platform using the 2.0 chemistry (PacBio). Meantime, an Illumina paired-end library with insert size of ~450 bp was constructed using the Illumina Genomic DNA Sample Preparation kit following the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina), and a Hi-C library was prepared following the proximo Hi-C plant protocol (Phase Genomics). Both the Illumina paired-end and the Hi-C libraries were sequenced on an Illumina NextSeq 500 platform with the paired-end mode and read length of 150 bp.</p><p>Nanopore sequences were generated for the SP accession LA1589 using the method described in Mazo-Molina et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Mazo-Molina, C. et al. Ptr1 evolved convergently with RPS2 and Mr5 to mediate recognition of AvrRpt2 in diverse solanaceous species. Plant J. 103, 1433–1445 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1582">45</a></sup> and used for validation of SV genotyping. Briefly, Nanopore libraries were constructed from the HMW DNA of LA1589 using the Ligation Sequencing Kit (SQK-LSK109) and sequenced on the MinION R9 flow cells for 48 h. Basecalling was performed using Guppy (v3.1.5).</p><p>Total RNA was extracted from immature, mature green and red ripe fruits using the QIAGEN RNeasy Plant Mini Kit (QIAGEN). Strand-specific RNA-Seq libraries were constructed using the protocol described in Zhong et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Zhong, S. et al. High-throughput Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing library preparation. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 2011, 940–949 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1589">46</a></sup> and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform (Illumina). Three biological replicates were performed for each sample.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">De novo assembly of the LA2093 genome</h3><p>Raw PacBio reads were error corrected and assembled into contigs using CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1601">47</a></sup> (v1.7.1) with default parameters except that ‘OvlMerThreshold’ and ‘corOutCoverage’ were set to 500 and 200, respectively. PacBio reads were then aligned to the contigs and based on the alignments errors in the assembled contigs were corrected using the Arrow program implemented in SMRT-link-5.1 (PacBio). Furthermore, the Illumina paired-end reads were processed to remove adaptor and low-quality sequences using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1605">48</a></sup> (v0.36). The cleaned Illumina reads were aligned to the contigs using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1609">49</a></sup> (v0.7.17) with default parameters, and based on the alignments two rounds of iterative error corrections were performed using Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1613">50</a></sup> (v1.22) with parameters ‘–fix bases–diploid’. The final error-corrected contigs were then compared against the NCBI non‐redundant nucleotide database, and those with more than 95% of their length similar to sequences of organelles (mitochondrion or chloroplast) or microorganisms (bacteria/fungi/viruses), were considered contaminants and discarded. The redundans pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Pryszcz, L. P. &amp; Gabaldon, T. Redundans: an assembly pipeline for highly heterozygous genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e113 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1617">51</a></sup> (v0.14a) was then used to remove redundancies in the assembled contigs with parameters ‘--identity 0.99 --overlap 0.97’.</p><p>To scaffold the assembled contigs, Illumina reads from the Hi-C library were processed with Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1624">48</a></sup> (v0.36) to remove adaptor and low-quality sequences. The cleaned Hi-C reads were aligned to the assembled contigs and the alignments were filtered using the Arima-HiC mapping pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/ArimaGenomics/mapping_pipeline">https://github.com/ArimaGenomics/mapping_pipeline</a>). Based on the alignments, the contigs were clustered into pseudomolecules using SALSA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ghurye, J. et al. Integrating Hi-C links with assembly graphs for chromosome-scale assembly. PLoS Comput Biol. 15, e1007273 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1635">52</a></sup> (v2.2) with parameters ‘-e GATC -i 3’. Furthermore, contigs of LA2093 were also assembled into pseudomolecules by comparing them with the Heinz1706 reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hosmani, P. S. et al. An improved de novo assembly and annotation of the tomato reference genome using single-molecule sequencing, Hi-C proximity ligation and optical maps. bioRxiv. Preprint at &#xA;                  https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/767764v1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1639">20</a></sup> (version 4.0) using RaGOO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Alonge, M. et al. RaGOO: fast and accurate reference-guided scaffolding of draft genomes. Genome Biol. 20, 224 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1643">6</a></sup> (v1.1). Inconsistencies between pseudomolecules constructed using the Hi-C data and those using the synteny information with the Heinz1706 genome were identified. The mis-joined scaffolds were manually corrected based on the Hi-C contact information, genome synteny information, and a genetic map constructed from a recombinant inbred line (RIL) population with LA2093 as one of the parents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Gonda, I. et al. Sequencing-based bin map construction of a tomato mapping population, facilitating high-resolution quantitative trait loci detection. Plant Genome 12, 1–4 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1648">16</a></sup>, resulting a consensus set of LA2093 pseudomolecules. Finally, the genetic map was also used to validate the final consensus set of LA2093 pseudomolecules using ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1652">53</a></sup> (v0.8.12). Inconsistencies between the LA2093 pseudomolecules and genetic maps were also manually checked and the accuracy of the LA2093 pseudomolecules was further validated using PacBio read alignment information.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Annotation of the LA2093 genome assembly</h3><p>MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1664">54</a></sup> (v11-2011) and LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1668">55</a></sup> (v1.5.10) were used to de novo identify miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs) and long terminal repeats (LTRs), respectively, in the assembled LA2093 genome. The LA2093 genome was masked using RepeatMasker (v4.0.8; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) with the identified MITEs and LTRs, and the unmasked genome sequences were then analyzed using RepeatModeler (v1.0.11; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>) to build a de novo repeat library. The final repeat library was obtained by combining the MITEs, LTRs and the de novo repeat library, and subsequently used to screen the LA2093 genome for repeat sequences using RepeatMasker. The identified repeat sequences were classified using the RepeatClassifier program of RepeatModeler.</p><p>Protein-coding genes were predicted from the repeat-masked LA2093 genome. LA2093 RNA-Seq reads generated in this study and from a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Gonda, I. et al. Sequencing-based bin map construction of a tomato mapping population, facilitating high-resolution quantitative trait loci detection. Plant Genome 12, 1–4 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1689">16</a></sup> were processed to trim low-quality and adapter sequences using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1693">48</a></sup> (v0.36). The cleaned high-quality RNA-Seq reads were aligned to the assembled genome using HISAT2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Kim, D., Paggi, J. M., Park, C., Bennett, C. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Graph-based genome alignment and genotyping with HISAT2 and HISAT-genotype. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 907–915 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1697">56</a></sup>) (v2.1) with default parameters. Transcripts were assembled from the read alignments using StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1701">57</a></sup> (v1.3.3b). The complete coding sequences (CDS) were predicted from the assembled transcripts using the PASA pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1705">58</a></sup> (v2.3.3). Ab initio gene predictions were performed using BRAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Hoff, K. J., Lomsadze, A., Borodovsky, M. &amp; Stanke, M. Whole-genome annotation with BRAKER. Methods Mol. Biol. 1962, 65–95 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1710">59</a></sup> (v2.0.4), GeneMark-ET<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Lomsadze, A., Burns, P. D. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Integration of mapped RNA-Seq reads into automatic training of eukaryotic gene finding algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, e119 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1714">60</a></sup> (v4.61), and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1718">61</a></sup> (v2006-07-28). Protein sequences from the Swiss-Prot database and from Heinz 1706, <i>S. pennellii</i>, pepper and potato were used as protein homology evidence. Finally, the high-confidence gene models in the LA2093 genome were predicted using the Maker pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1725">62</a></sup> (v2.31.10) by integrating ab initio predictions, transcript mapping and protein homology evidence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Detection of SVs and SNPs between reference genomes</h3><p>To identity SVs between genomes of LA2093 and Heinz 1706 (SL4.0), the two genomes were first aligned using Minimap2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094–3100 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1737">63</a></sup>) (v2.17) with the parameter ‘-ax asm5’. The resulting alignments were analyzed using Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021–3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1741">64</a></sup> (v1.1) for SV identification. The identified SVs spanning or close (&lt;50 bp) to gap regions in either of the two genomes were excluded.</p><p>SVs were also identified using pbsv (v2.2; <a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/pbsv">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/pbsv</a>) and SVIM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Heller, D. &amp; Vingron, M. SVIM: structural variant identification using mapped long reads. Bioinformatics 35, 2907–2915 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1755">65</a></sup> (v1.2.0) by aligning LA2093 PacBio reads to the Heinz1706 genome and Heinz 1706 PacBio reads to the LA2093 genome. The identified SVs spanning gap regions in the genomes were discarded. For large inversions identified through direct comparison of the two genomes, the two breakpoints of a candidate inversion were identified based on the genome alignment results. To confirm the breakpoints, the supported split reads were extracted from the results of pbsv and SVIM. Inversions were kept if more than 90% of total reads spanning the breakpoint were split reads or they were detected by both pbsv and SVIM. For the remaining SVs, the 5-kb flanking sequences of each SV were extracted from the reference genome, and then blasted against the query genome. The blast hits were then compared to the unique alignments between LA2093 and Heinz 1706 genomes identified by Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021–3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1759">64</a></sup>. An SV was kept if the following criteria were met: (1) the blast hits of the two flank sequences of the SV (alignment length &gt;50 bp, identity &gt;90%, e-value &lt;1e−10) were in the expected region on the query genome; and (2) the distance between the two blast hits was largely consistent with the SV size estimated by PacBio read mapping. For insertions, we required that the difference in SV size determined by PacBio read mapping and distance between the two blast hits was smaller than 20% of the estimated SV size. For deletions, the allowed gaps or overlaps between the two blast hits of flanking regions should be smaller than 3 bp. Repeat expansions/contractions and tandem expansions/contractions detected by Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021–3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1763">64</a></sup> were converted into one or more simple indels if the precise breakpoints were defined using the SVs identified by pbsv. SVs identified by both Assemblytics and pbsv were combined if they overlapped with each other (&gt;50% in each). GO term enrichment analysis for the SV-related genes was performed using the Fisher’s exact test in the Blast2GO suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Gotz, S. et al. High-throughput functional annotation and data mining with the Blast2GO suite. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, 3420–3435 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1767">66</a></sup> (v1.3.11) with a cutoff of adjusted <i>P</i> value &lt;0.05.</p><p>SNPs between the two genomes were identified by comparing LA2093 and Heinz 1706 genomes using MUMmer4 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Marcais, G. et al. MUMmer4: A fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1778">67</a></sup>) (v4.0.0). The uniquely aligned fragments were used to identify SNPs with the show-snp tool in the MUMmer4 package.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">SV and SNP genotyping in the tomato population</h3><p>Genome resequencing data of 725 tomato accessions reported in Gao et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1791">17</a></sup> were downloaded from the NCBI SRA database (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM10">6</a>). The downloaded raw Illumina sequences from each accession were first processed to consolidated duplicated read pairs, which were defined as those having identical bases in the first 90 bp (for 100-bp reads) or 100 bp (for 150-bp reads) of both left and right reads, into unique read pairs. The resulting reads were processed to trim adaptor and low-quality sequences using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1798">48</a></sup> (v0.36).</p><p>The high-quality reference SVs identified between the LA2093 and Heinz1706 genomes were genotyped in the 725 tomato accessions. The cleaned Illumina reads from each accession were aligned to the LA2093 and Heinz1706 genomes, respectively, using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1805">49</a></sup> (v0.7.17) allowing no more than 3% mismatches. For each SV in each accession, reads aligned to regions spanning the breakpoints of the SV in both LA2093 and Heinz1706 genomes were extracted and checked. For inversions, only split reads were used as evidence and each breakpoint was supported by at least three split reads. For indels with breakpoints supported by less than three split reads, the read coverage in the deleted regions were further checked. For an indel, we required that &lt;50% of the deleted region was covered by reads with 2× depth, while &gt;50% of at least one of the flanking regions with the same length of the deleted region was covered. Based on the split read and read depth information, SVs in a particular accession were classified as the LA2093 genotype (same as in LA2093), the Heinz1706 genotype (same as in Heinz 1706), heterozygous (containing both LA2093 and Heinz1706 alleles), or undetermined (genotypes that could not be determined due to insufficient read mapping information). Accessions with less than 40% of SVs genotyped were excluded, leaving 597 tomato accessions kept for the downstream analyses.</p><p>To evaluate the accuracy of our SV genotyping using short reads, the reference SVs were also genotyped in LA1589 using Nanopore long reads. The Nanopore reads were first self-corrected using NECAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Chen, Y. et al. Fast and accurate assembly of nanopore reads via progressive error correction and adaptive read selection. bioRxiv. Preprint at &#xA;                  https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/2020.02.01.930107v1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1812">68</a></sup> (v0.0.1) and then further corrected using Illumina reads with fmlrc<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Wang, J. R., Holt, J., McMillan, L. &amp; Jones, C. D. FMLRC: Hybrid long read error correction using an FM-index. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 50 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1816">69</a></sup> (v0.1.2). The corrected long reads were aligned to the LA2093 and Heinz1706 genomes, respectively, using Minimap2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094–3100 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1820">63</a></sup>) (v2.17). Based on the alignments, SVs were called using SVIM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Heller, D. &amp; Vingron, M. SVIM: structural variant identification using mapped long reads. Bioinformatics 35, 2907–2915 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1824">65</a></sup> (v1.2.0) and compared with those genotyped using short reads in LA1589 to estimate the accuracy of SV genotyping.</p><p>Illumina reads aligned to the LA2093 genome were used for SNP calling. First, the duplicated alignments were marked using Picard (v2.3; <a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>), with the parameter ‘OPTICAL_DUPLICATE_PIXEL_DISTANCE = 250’. The ‘HaplotypeCaller’ function of GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1838">70</a></sup> (v3.8) was then used to generate a GVCF file for each accession with parameters ‘--genotyping_mode DISCOVERY --max_alternate_alleles 3 --read_filter OverclippedRead’, followed by the population variant calling using the function ‘GenotypeGVCFs’ with default parameters. Hard filtering was applied to the raw variant calling set, with parameters ‘QD &lt; 2.0 | | FS &gt; 60.0 | | MQ &lt; 40.0 | | MQRankSum &lt; −12.5 | | ReadPosRankSum &lt; −8.0’. Sites with at least 50% accessions genotyped and minor allele frequency (MAF) ≥ 0.03 and overlapping with the identified SNPs from the alignments of LA2093 and Heinz1706 genomes were kept.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Population genomic analyses</h3><p>Maximum-likelihood phylogenetic trees were constructed for the tomato accessions using the full SV dataset and SNPs at four-fold degenerated sites, respectively, using IQ-TREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Nguyen, L. T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1850">71</a></sup> (v1.6.8) with 1000 bootstraps. Population structure was investigated using FastStructure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Raj, A., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. fastSTRUCTURE: variational inference of population structure in large SNP data sets. Genetics 197, 573–589 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1854">72</a></sup> with default parameters. To analyze the selection of SVs during domestication, improvement and modern breeding, the frequency of each allele of a particular SV in each group was calculated. Significance of the difference of the frequencies between two compared groups was determined using Fisher’s exact test. The resulting raw <i>P</i> values of SVs were then corrected using the Bonferroni method and SVs with corrected <i>P</i> values &lt;0.001 were defined as those under selection. The nucleotide diversity and population differentiation index (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) across the genome were calculated using VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1869">73</a></sup> (v0.1.16) based on the SVs and SNPs. <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values were calculated in each 1000-kb window with a step size of 250 kb.</p><p>Putative introgressions between two groups were identified using a likelihood ratio test<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="McNally, K. L. et al. Genomewide SNP variation reveals relationships among landraces and modern varieties of rice. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 12273–12278 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1880">74</a></sup> with the SV datasets. For each SV site in an accession of the heirloom or modern group, the percentage of accessions sharing this SV genotype was first derived in each of the two groups, then the ratio of the percentage of accessions sharing the genotype in the group this specific accession belonging to that of accessions in the SP group was calculated. The average ratio for all SV sites in each of the 1000-kb windows with a step size of 250 kb were obtained. Regions with ratios of 0.9 or less and containing two or more SVs were defined as introgressions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RNA-Seq expression and eQTL analysis</h3><p>Raw RNA-Seq data of 399 accessions reported in Zhu et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhu, G. et al. Rewiring of the fruit metabolome in tomato breeding. Cell 172, 249–261.e12. (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1892">36</a></sup> and of LA2093 and NC EBR-1 at four different stages of fruit development reported in Gao et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gao, L. et al. The tomato pan-genome uncovers new genes and a rare allele regulating fruit flavor. Nat. Genet. 51, 1044–1051 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1896">17</a></sup> were downloaded from the NCBI SRA database under the accessions PRJNA396272 and PRJNA659593, respectively. Raw sequencing reads were processed to trim low-quality and adaptor sequences using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1900">48</a></sup>. The cleaned reads were aligned to the LA2093 genome using HISAT2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Kim, D., Paggi, J. M., Park, C., Bennett, C. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Graph-based genome alignment and genotyping with HISAT2 and HISAT-genotype. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 907–915 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1904">56</a></sup>) (v2.1.0). Based on the alignments, raw read counts were derived for each gene and normalized to fragments per kilobase transcripts per million mapped fragments (FPKM). For eQTL analysis, expression data from 305 out of the 399 accessions that had SV data in this study were used. Genes with a median FPKM value of zero were excluded from the downstream analysis. Principal component analysis was performed based on the FPKM values, and nine accessions with FPKM values greater than 2.5 standard deviations from the mean in any of the first three principal components were excluded from the downstream analysis. To obtain a normal distribution of expression values for each gene, FPKM values of each gene were further normalized using the quantile-quantile normalization (qqnorm) function in R. To identify the hidden and confounding factors in the expression data, the normal quantile transformed expression values were processed using the probabilistic estimation of expression residuals (PEER) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Stegle, O., Parts, L., Piipari, M., Winn, J. &amp; Durbin, R. Using probabilistic estimation of expression residuals (PEER) to obtain increased power and interpretability of gene expression analyses. Nat. Protoc. 7, 500–507 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1908">75</a></sup>. The first 20 factors were selected as additional covariates in the genome-wide association studies. The Balding-Nichols kinship matrix constructed using EMMAX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Kang, H. M. et al. Variance component model to account for sample structure in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 42, 348–354 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1913">76</a></sup> (v20120210) with all SNPs and SVs was used to correct population structure. The missing genotypes in the raw biallelic SV dataset were imputed using the k-nearest neighbor (KNN) algorithm implemented in the fillGenotype software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Huang, X. et al. Genome-wide association studies of 14 agronomic traits in rice landraces. Nat. Genet. 42, 961–967 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1917">77</a></sup>. In order to obtain the optimal imputation accuracy and filling rate, the accession with fewest missing genotypes in SP, SLC and Heirloom were selected and 10%, 20%, and 30% SV sites were randomly masked as missing genotypes for imputing. The imputations were performed using the fillGenotype with the combinations of the parameters: <i>w</i> = 20, 30, 50, 65 or 80), <i>p</i> = −3, −5, −7 or −9, k = 3, 5, 7, or 9), and <i>r</i> = 0.65, 0.7, 0.75, and 0.8. The optimal combination of parameters (<i>w</i> = 80, <i>k</i> = 3, <i>p</i> = −7, <i>r</i> = 0.8) was selected after comparing the filling rate and imputation accuracy of each combination of the parameters (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">5</a>). Only biallelic imputed SVs with minor allele frequency ≥ 1% and missing data rate ≤ 40% (a total of 71,684 SVs) were used for eQTL analysis. Genome-wide associations of transformed expression were estimated using the linear mixed model implemented in EMMAX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Kang, H. M. et al. Variance component model to account for sample structure in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 42, 348–354 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1946">76</a></sup> (v20120210). The Bonferroni test criteria at α = 0.05 and α = 1 were used as thresholds for significant and suggestive associations between variations and traits (expression), respectively, as described in Li et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Li, M. X., Yeung, J. M., Cherny, S. S. &amp; Sham, P. C. Evaluating the effective numbers of independent tests and significant p-value thresholds in commercial genotyping arrays and public imputation reference datasets. Hum. Genet. 131, 747–756 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1950">78</a></sup>. In this study, the Bonferroni-corrected thresholds for the <i>P</i> values were 6.97 × 10<sup>−7</sup> at α = 0.05 and 1.40 × 10<sup>−5</sup> at α = 1, with corresponding −log10(<i>P</i>) values of 6.15 and 4.85, respectively.</p><p>Linkage disequilibrium (LD) decay was measured by calculating correlation coefficients (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) for all pairs of SVs within 10 Mb using PopLDdecay<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Zhang, C., Dong, S. S., Xu, J. Y., He, W. M. &amp; Yang, T. L. PopLDdecay: a fast and effective tool for linkage disequilibrium decay analysis based on variant call format files. Bioinformatics 35, 1786–1788 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1972">79</a></sup> (v3.27) with parameters ‘-MaxDist 500 -MAF 0.05 -Het 0.88 -Miss 0.999’. The stable <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> value was considered as the background level of LD. The background level of LD and physical distance were 0.205 and 1.94 Mb in this study, respectively. SVs within close vicinity to each other and associated with the expression of the same gene were grouped into an eQTL block, represented by the most significant SV (the lead SV) in the block. Two adjacent SVs were grouped into one region if the <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> &gt; 0.205 and physical distance &lt;1.94 Mb, and regions with at least three significant SVs were considered as candidate eQTL blocks. eQTLs were considered cis if they located within 50 kb of transcription start sites or transcription stop sites of the corresponding genes; otherwise, the eQTLs were considered trans.</p><p>eQTL hotspots were identified using the hot_scan software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Silva, I. T., Rosales, R. A., Holanda, A. J., Nussenzweig, M. C. &amp; Jankovic, M. Identification of chromosomal translocation hotspots via scan statistics. Bioinformatics 30, 2551–2558 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1987">80</a></sup> with a window size of 50 kb and an adjusted <i>P</i>-value less than 0.05. Pairwise Pearson correlation coefficients between the target genes and genes located inside the eQTL hotspots were calculated using the FPKM values. The candidate master regulator was identified for each eQTL hotspot using the iterative group analysis (iGA) approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Breitling, R., Amtmann, A. &amp; Herzyk, P. Iterative Group Analysis (iGA): a simple tool to enhance sensitivity and facilitate interpretation of microarray experiments. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 34 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d30283e1994">81</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">Supplementary information</a> files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#MOESM1">Supplementary information</a> file. The datasets and plant materials generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. Raw genome and transcriptome sequencing reads have been deposited into the NCBI BioProject database under accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA607731">PRJNA607731</a>. The genome assembly of <i>Solanum pimpinellifolium</i> LA2093 has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAAONP000000000.1/">JAAONP000000000</a>. The genome sequence of LA2093 and the associated annotations are also available at Sol Genomics Network (<a href="https://solgenomics.net/">https://solgenomics.net/</a>). The SV genotyping data in the 597 tomato accessions is available via Figshare [<a href="https://figshare.com/articles/dataset/tomato_SV_matrix/12922151">https://figshare.com/articles/dataset/tomato_SV_matrix/12922151</a>].  <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19682-0#Sec19">Source data</a> are provided with this paper.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>Computational pipelines related to SV detection and genotyping are available at GitHub [<a href="https://github.com/GaoLei-bio/SV">https://github.com/GaoLei-bio/SV</a><u>]</u>.</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5560543" aria-label="View reference 9 on PubMed Central" rel="nofollow">PubMed Central</a>
